                                        <div class="single-content-holder">
                        <div class="author">
        By <a href="http://www.stereogum.com/author/michael-n/">Michael Nelson</a> <span class="date">/ June 20, 2013</span>
                                </div>
                    <div class="article-content clearfix">
                                                    <p>Time has a marvelous ability to render the absurd familiar. Mankind can harvest vast energy from the basic stuff of matter, using means that only people with considerable higher education can even describe accurately. But nobodyâs taken aback by this fact because, welp, theyâve been doing that shit since your grandpaâs time. Humans can fly! We cured smallpox! You can talk to anyone on the planet instantaneously! These facts are insane when you think about them, but nobody does because weâre used to them. (<a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=KpUNA2nutbk">My favorite Louis C.K. bit</a> deals with this phenomenon, but I digress.)<!--more--></p>
<p>In the same way, Pink Floyd â one of the most popular musical groups of all time â has been normalized by their ubiquity. Their record sales figures alone boggle the mind. According to Wikipedia, theyâve sold roughly 250 million albums.</p>
<p>The human brain tends to gloss over numbers of that size when theyâre delivered without context, so hereâs some. In 1990, there were just under 250 million people living in the United States of America. Pink Floyd has effectively sold an album to <em>every single one</em> of those people. That puts them ahead of Celine Dion, AC/DC, the Rolling Stones, and ABBA, among others. Only the Beatles, Elvis, Michael Jackson, Led Zeppelin, Madonna, and Elton John have done better.Â  And that figure doesnât include all of the people whoâve pirated their albums, or who never bought their albums but have heard their music on the radio for decades, or whoâve lived with family members and spouses who played Pink Floyd in their homes, and so on. Their cultural reach is epically, imperially huge.</p>
<p>And that reach does not end with their album and merchandise sales, which continue to pile up almost 20 years after their demise. Pink Floydâs songs have been <a href="https://www.youtube.com/results?q=brick+in+the+wall+parody&amp;um=1&amp;ie=UTF-8&amp;sa=N&amp;tab=w1">covered, referenced, and parodied</a> into oblivion. Planetarium laser shows set to their music are so popular that theyâve become a stoner clichÃ©. The <a href="http://janieo89.files.wordpress.com/2010/03/pink-floyd1.jpg">poster of six naked women</a> with Floyd album covers painted on their backs is a dorm-room staple, rivaling DalÃ­âs <a href="http://0.tqn.com/d/arthistory/1/0/l/i/dali_moma_0708_11.jpg"><em>The Persistence of Memory</em></a> and the <a href="http://imgc.allpostersimages.com/images/P-473-488-90/21/2185/BEQCD00Z/posters/scarface.jpg">Scarface</a> film poster in popularity. Even Pink Floyd tribute acts do incredibly well: Easy Star All Starsâ dub reggae revision of <em>The</em> <em>Dark Side of the Moon</em> held onto a spot in the reggae charts for five years.</p>
<p>This band is so popular, and has been for so long, that itâs basically impossible to imagine the rock/pop landscape without them. Theyâre like Isaac Asimov â many of their tropes seem hackneyed now, but thatâs because <em>everyone has been ripping them off for decades.</em></p>
<p>And yet, in spite of their impossible sales and disarming omnipresence, Pink Floyd might be the most unlikely success story in rock history â an extremely dark, thoroughly experimental group that began their career with a madman at the helm and with a (<a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_UFslIWgyS4">partially media-devised</a>) affinity for scary underground drugs. The list of 60s-era pop music diktats they gleefully violated over the course of their career is too long to enumerate, but hereâs a partial version:</p>
<p><em>â Do not write 25-minute songs.</em></p>
<p><em>â Do not devote entire albums to strange formal experiments.</em></p>
<p><em>â Do not neglect to release a single for much of your peak creative period.</em></p>
<p><em>â Do not go crazy.</em></p>
<p><em>â Do not sing about going crazy.</em></p>
<p><em>â Do not bum everyone out with harsh critiques of your society.</em></p>
<p><em>â Do not </em><a href="http://www.brain-damage.co.uk/concerts/pink-floyd-olympic-stadium-montreal-canada-july-6th-1977-2.htmlb"><em>spit on your fans</em></a><em>, even if theyâre being rude.</em></p>
<p>And this stuff is just the beginning. Even by rock-god standards, Pink Floyd were sublimely bizarre, both as an artistic entity and as a group of people. Though itâs impossible to imagine the 2013 rock/pop landscape without them, itâs just as tough to imagine an equally daring group blowing up today.</p>
<p>So how did Pink Floyd get so immensely popular? Some of their success can be traced to timing and serendipity. They formed in 1965, when rockâs frontiers were wild and unexplored â there was still plenty of territory for them to pioneer. Their association with the psychedelic drug scene, accidental though it may have been, helped make them a youth-culture buzz band. Chief lyricist/bassist Roger Watersâs turn towards social and psychological ruminations in the 1970s resonated with the widespread societal soul-searching of the era. Exogenous market forces helped as well, of course. Music culture was more homogenized back then, and people still bought records.</p>
<p>None of this is to suggest that Pink Floyd succeeded the way they did <em>mostly</em> because of luck or timing. The main reason that Pink Floyd is so beloved â and this fact sometimes gets lost amidst the heaps of cultural baggage and ephemera that surround them â is because they were a <em>fucking great band</em>. Itâs impossible for fans to even describe their sound without spewing breathless superlatives, so I wonât go down that road. (Yet.) After all, you already know what Pink Floyd sounds like. Who doesnât?</p>
<p>This week, the entire Pink Floyd catalog was made available on streaming-music service Spotify, so it&#8217;s an apt opportunity for us to cull that catalog and come up with a list of the ten best Pink Floyd songs. Uh, about that: Iâm not sure these are the ten best Pink Floyd songs. Hell, Iâm not even sure these are my own ten favorite Pink Floyd songs.</p>
<p>This band released 14 albums. Some of those albums focus on oddball experimentation â <em>Atom Heart Mother</em>, <em>Ummagumma</em>, and the adapted film scores <em>More</em> and <em>Obscured By Clouds</em>. Some of them came out during transitional or waning periods in the bandâs discography â <em>Saucerful Of Secrets</em>, <em>The Final Cut</em>, <em>A Momentary Lapse Of Reason</em>, and <em>The Division Bell</em>. These albums are (mostly) good, but theyâre outshone by the towering brilliance of the remaining six: <em>The Piper At The Gates of Dawn</em>, <em>Meddle</em>, <em>The Dark Side Of The Moon</em>, <em>Wish You Were Here</em>, <em>Animals</em>, and <em>The Wall</em>. Big swathes of these six albums deserve to be included. To make matters more complicated, several of them are organized into unified suites that donât break down easily. I considered including all of <em>Dark Side</em> and <em>The Wall</em> as single tunes, but thatâd be a cop-out. I also elected not to unify non-consecutive suites, so you wonât see âShine On You Crazy Diamond I-IXâ or âAnother Brick In The Wall (Parts 1-3).â</p>
<p>Ultimately, I chose the entries based on whether Iâd be able to forgive myself for excluding them. (Even that didnât really do the trick, but in the face of deadlines, ugly decisions must be made.) In the process, I left out a lot of choice cuts from the Floyd catalog. Put my picks on trial in the comments.</p>
<h2>10. âAstronomy Domineâ (from <em>The Piper At The Gates Of Dawn</em>, 1967)</h2>
<p>Even 46 years after the release of âAstronomy Domine,â it remains one of the oddest tunes ever to launch the recording career of popular rock band. The vocals â usually the focal point of a rock song, especially at the time â consist of guitarist Syd Barrett and keyboardist Richard Wright chanting nonsense verse about space, which is weird enough on its own. Their surroundings get even spookier: Nick Masonâs staggering leadoff fill, Barrettâs pinched-nerve intro riff, the spiraling chromatic chorus, and producer Norman Smithâs encompassing collage of astral beeps and echo-effect clatter.</p>
<p>Despite being the only Pink Floyd song that lyrically deals directly with space, âAstronomy Domineâ played a huge role in launching <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_rock">space rock</a>, which remains in orbit today. Itâs also significant because itâs the most succinct expression of Barrettâs spectacular but short-lived chemistry with Floyd. The drug-induced breakdown that eventually forced him from the band would color their workâs emotional character for the remainder of their run.</p>
<p><iframe src="https://embed.spotify.com/?uri=spotify:track:2iEKKLe3gqqm2EmXbMq8cy" width="350" height="90" frameborder="0" allowtransparency="true"></iframe></p>
<h2>9. âEchoesâ (from <em>Meddle</em>, 1971)</h2>
<p>A buddy of mine used to play a fun game with this tune. When he was in college, heâd go to the local watering hole, which had a copy of <em>Meddle</em> in its jukebox. Heâd pay for 12 songs, the first of which was always the 24-minute âEchoes.â The bartender would become agitated as soon as its opening chime cut through the air. The playlist would then return to a normal roster of boozy bar jams, until it reached its last songâ¦which was, of course, always âEchoes.â</p>
<p>This isnât Pink Floydâs longest song; that honor goes to the ungainly but beautiful title track from <em>Atom Heart Mother</em>. It is, however, their first wholly successful effort at writing and recording a side-length multipart epic. Conceived in pieces and composed collaboratively by the entire band, âEchoesâ cleared the path for the ambitious full-album compositions that Pink Floyd would crank out for the rest of the decade.</p>
<p>Fittingly, âEchoesâ has echoed in other famous musical works. Check out Masonâs robotically crisp snare/closed-hat attack starting at around 7:30 of the killer <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=PGwPSPIhohk"><em>Live at Pompeii</em> version</a>, which foreshadows Phil Selwayâs drum tone on Radioheadâs <em>OK Computer</em>. Even more uncanny is the similarity between the songâs instrumental refrain and the overture from Andrew Lloyd Weberâs 1986 musical <em>The Phantom Of The Opera</em>. Waters elected not to file plagiarism litigation over the matter because, as he put it, âLifeâs too long to bother with suing Andrew fucking Lloyd Weber.â</p>
<p><iframe src="https://embed.spotify.com/?uri=spotify:track:6nfXfoFwVyQZ6AJjokeFzi" frameborder="0" width="350" height="90"></iframe></p>
<h2>8. âComfortably Numbâ (from <em>The Wall</em>, 1979)</h2>
<p><em>The Wall </em>rightly has a reputation as the first of Roger Watersâs two quasi-solo albums under the Pink Floyd name. Ironically, one of its best cuts involved only a gentle touch from his creative hand. âComfortably Numbâ is mostly a David Gilmour tune; Waters contributed only lyrics and a snippet of chord work. (He also mumbles Dylan-style through the verses; the rest of the vocals are all Gilmour.)</p>
<p>People often confuse âComfortably Numbâ for a song about drug addiction, but Watersâs real lyrical inspiration was a 1977 incident in which he was forced to soldier through a lengthy gig in Philly while under the doctor-directed influence of tranquilizers. The real story on this tune, though, is Gilmourâs two legendary guitar solos. On the eve of the 80s, he had adopted a steely lead tone that foreshadowed the arena-metal flash of the decade to come. Of course, thereâs no self-indulgent shredding here â just Gilmourâs pointillistic precision and impeccable phrasing, which are more than enough to set this drifting song ablaze.</p>
<p><iframe src="https://embed.spotify.com/?uri=spotify:track:082cLCIXNPg2ruTrENz4Vt" width="350" height="90" frameborder="0" allowtransparency="true"></iframe></p>
<h2>7. âMoneyâ (from <em>The Dark Side Of The Moon</em>, 1973)</h2>
<p>âMoneyâ is a rite of passage. Whenever a young lad or lass picks up a bass guitar and learns the ways of rock ânâ roll rhythm, there comes a time when he or she must grapple with the challenge of odd time signatures. And when that time comes, the bass line from âMoneyâ awaits.</p>
<p>Though the most iconic features of âMoneyâ are its 7/4 verse rhythm and its opening tape loop, everything else in the song is equally great. Watersâs lyrics ridicule middle-class greed â a theme he would memorably return to several times on the albums that followed <em>Dark Side</em>. Wright and Gilmour dart in and out of view during the songâs first half, adding sting to the rhythm sectionâs sardonic funk. The lengthy lead section is outrageousâsession sax man Dick Perry gets things cooking with a wheedling blues solo, which in turn sets up an explosive wet-dry-wet sequence from Gilmour. Even Mason kinda rages out beneath Gilmourâs last solo, flaunting the chops that hide behind his typically spare, metronomic timekeeping.</p>
<p>âMoneyâ is a rare show of pure force on a largely abstract album; no wonder that itâs become one of Pink Floydâs most omnipresent tunes. But itâs not quite as omnipresent asâ¦</p>
<p><iframe src="https://embed.spotify.com/?uri=spotify:track:3aoDEt6zSuYQ47gzarlaVo" width="350" height="90" frameborder="0" allowtransparency="true"></iframe></p>
<h2>6. âThe Happiest Days of Our Lives/Another Brick in the Wall (Part 2)â (from <em>The Wall</em>, 1979)</h2>
<p>This two-song sequence is so famous and popular, so thoroughly internalized and referenced and joked around with and bitched about, that itâs almost hard to think about as actual <em>music</em>. Itâs more of a cultural artifact now â a collectively-owned heirloom, like <em>The Great Gatsby</em> or <em>Star Wars</em>.</p>
<p>Ironically, even the members of Pink Floyd agree that âHappiest Days/Another Brick (Part 2)â doesnât really sound like a Pink Floyd song. Two of its most identifiable features â Masonâs four-on-the-floor beat and the gang of children who sing the second chorus â were included at the behest of producer Bob Ezrin. Waters, who despised disco, found both ideas distasteful until he heard the fully assembled version of the song.</p>
<p>And it is good that he relented. Had he followed through on his original desire to write a shorter song expressing his frustration with Britainâs schooling system, the world wouldâve been deprived of one of its greatest anti-authoritarian youth anthems. Even after decades of endless critical dissection and commercial overexposure, âHappiest Days/Another Brick (Part 2)â remains a song of incredible resonance. We still donât need no thought control.</p>
<p><iframe src="https://embed.spotify.com/?uri=spotify:track:20OBAaMQuI0ox3CE3h2wGX" width="350" height="90" frameborder="0" allowtransparency="true"></iframe></p>
<p><iframe src="https://embed.spotify.com/?uri=spotify:track:3fuXKiEzDCs1IBnLHQ5CL0" width="350" height="90" frameborder="0" allowtransparency="true"></iframe></p>
<h2>5. âPigs (Three Different Ones)â (from <em>Animals</em>, 1977)</h2>
<p>By the mid-70s, punk rock had begun to take hold in the United Kingdom. Psychedelic drugs were out; huffing glue and punching cops were in. <a href="http://24.media.tumblr.com/tumblr_m35crjA5t81r6qzc5o1_500.jpg">Punk musicians</a> and fans routinely slammed Floyd as exemplars of contemporary rockâs dowdy politics and sonic flabbiness.</p>
<p>These criticisms purportedly influenced Floydâs work on <em>Animals</em>. You wouldnât know it by the music itself, which sounds stark but sprawls hard â roughly 39 of its 42 minutes are spread over just three songs. It also gives more space to Gilmourâs ever-glorious soloing, which is at its weirdest on âPigsâ â he uses a talk box to imitate the titular animalâs squeal, and even rips some fretless bass leads while Waters holds down the rhythm guitar.</p>
<p>The lyrics on <em>Animals</em> are a different story. Each of those three monster tunes compares a British social caste to an animal, and the comparisons are not flattering. âPigsâ snipes at upper-crusters who cynically exploit the insecurities of others: <em>âBus-stop rat bag, charade you are / You fucked-up old hag, charade you are / You radiate cold shafts of broken glass / Youâre nearly worth a laugh</em>,<em>â </em>Waters sneers during the second verse. His notorious spitting incident took place during a performance of the song.</p>
<p>Oink, oink.</p>
<p><iframe src="https://embed.spotify.com/?uri=spotify:track:4gSiNltIPWhEMtxN8gQ9VG" width="350" height="90" frameborder="0" allowtransparency="true"></iframe></p>
<h2>4. âShine On You Crazy Diamond (Parts I-V)â (from <em>Wish You Were Here</em>, 1975)</h2>
<p>The emotional aftershock of Syd Barrettâs crack-up reverberates through much of Pink Floydâs catalog, but only âShine On You Crazy Diamondâ addresses him directly. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wish_You_Were_Here_(Pink_Floyd_album)#Crazy_diamond">famous episode</a> in which Barrett â fat, bald, disoriented, and unrecognizable to his former friends â stopped by Abbey Road Studios unannounced in the midst of the songâs mixing process is one of the saddest and most poignant anecdotes in all of rock history. Waters, who considered Barrett an avatar for the ravaging effects of 20<sup>th</sup>-century society on the human soul, reputedly broke down in tears.</p>
<p>Floydâs great sorrow over Barrettâs fate seeps from every note of their group-composed tribute to him. Waters delivers some of his most direct and heartfelt lyrics over Masonâs 6/8 sway: â<em>Now thereâs a look in your eyes / Like black holes in the skyâ¦Come on you raver, you seer of visions / Come on you painter, you piper, you prisoner, and shine!</em>â Wright, rarely in the spotlight, opens the song with a beautiful synth collage and supports its dramatic climax with weeping chordal organs. And the Gilmour lead that starts around 6:10? Pretty sure thatâs the sound of manâs soul draining away.</p>
<p><iframe src="https://embed.spotify.com/?uri=spotify:track:2dWCp5vcNwSy25zRVFGC5t" width="350" height="90" frameborder="0" allowtransparency="true"></iframe></p>
<h2>3. âBrain Damage/Eclipseâ (from <em>The Dark Side Of The Moon</em>, 1973)</h2>
<p>These two songs are often collectively mistaken for the title track from <em>Dark Side</em> (there isnât one) â âBrain Damageâ uses the title as its chorus and runs seamlessly into the finale âEclipse.âÂ  Together, they provide the emotional zenith to one of the most seamlessly constructed albums of all time, and what a zenith it is.</p>
<p>Itâs basically impossible to address the emotional impact of âBrain Damage/Eclipseâ without discussing about the album as a whole, so instead Iâll say this: engineer Alan Parsons (yes, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Alan_Parsons_Project">that one</a>) achieved one of the great production feats of the analog era with these two tunes. The balance between spare, nervous verse and lush, organ-and-choir-driven chorus on âBrain Damageâ perfectly matches the lyrics, which see Waters agonize over his psychological similarity to his crazy ex-bandmate â <em>âAnd if your head explodes with dark forebodings too / Iâll see you on the dark side of the moon</em>.â And the way that the stately, all-encompassing swing of âEclipseâ collapses, with signature Pink Floyd circularity, into the heartbeat that opens the albumâ¦yeah, you can start to understand why this peculiar little record has sold well.</p>
<p><iframe src="https://embed.spotify.com/?uri=spotify:track:2gCf9PqIvpKbxwfJMBWwbn" width="350" height="90" frameborder="0" allowtransparency="true"></iframe></p>
<p><iframe src="https://embed.spotify.com/?uri=spotify:track:7Dla2S1irAtL9VjSUIapDO" width="350" height="90" frameborder="0" allowtransparency="true"></iframe></p>
<h2>2. âDogsâ (from <em>Animals</em>, 1977)</h2>
<p>This song holds a special place in my heart. The first time I heard it was the (17-minute-long) moment at which I realized that Pink Floyd was no dinosaur hippie group, but a musical and emotional powerhouse that will probably never stop being relevant as long as Western civilization remains a scary, tragic place.</p>
<p>Like the other big tracks on <em>Animals</em>, âDogsâ compares humanity to its eponymous beast. It boasts the most incisive and affecting lyrics of the three, though: a brutal examination of a conniving businessmanâs flaws and weaknesses. The final verse summarizes its subjectâs lifetime of abjection with such devastating precision that itâs tough to listen to. Only âShine On You Crazy Diamondâ matches it for sheer intensity.</p>
<p>âDogsâ also marks the career high-water mark for David Gilmourâs lead playing. Since Gilmour occupies a high seat in the rock-soloist canon, âDogsâ has a considerable claim to the title of Best Guitar Song Ever. (Got a better pick? Letâs hear it.) His playing on this song channels so much heart through such economical phrasing thatâ¦well, here come the superlatives. Just listen.</p>
<p><iframe src="https://embed.spotify.com/?uri=spotify:track:1kAvmwfFoSwfyfuW5RmfD0" width="350" height="90" frameborder="0" allowtransparency="true"></iframe></p>
<h2>1. âWish You Were Hereâ (from <em>Wish You Were Here</em>, 1975)</h2>
<p>Letâs be straight: every song on this list couldâve landed in the top slot. I chose âWish You Were Hereâ because itâs the strongest distillation of the traits that made this band great. For all of Pink Floydâs relentless progressivism â the superhuge songs, the rock operas, the musique concrÃ¨te influence, the zany stagecraft â they would have never have achieved so much if they werenât able to reign the weirdness in and write pristine songs. âThey write great songsâ is maybe the worst rock-writer clichÃ©, but for Pink Floyd, itâs inescapably true. And this one wasnât even a single!</p>
<p>âWish You Were Hereâ finds all of Floydâs finest attributes present and in perfect balance: sophisticated studio trickery, gorgeous instrumental textures, a timeless chord progression, restrained-but-powerful leads from Gilmour and Wright, a Syd Barrett nod (the <em>âcold steel railâ</em> line refers to a Barrett lyric from <em>The Madcap Laughs</em>) and a refrain that will lodge in your braincase the first time you hear it and stay there for the rest of your life. It manages this crazy achievement in just five minute and thirty-four seconds. And it organizes the whole affair around the emotion that defines Pink Floydâs best work: an aching, cosmic loneliness that every living person has felt at one time or another.</p>
<p>And THAT is why this weird-ass band has sold 250 million records.</p>
<p><iframe src="https://embed.spotify.com/?uri=spotify:track:1wHSxWBfPr3GO31y8KGrWe" width="350" height="90" frameborder="0" allowtransparency="true"></iframe></p>
<p>Listen to this playlist on Spotify <a href="http://open.spotify.com/user/stereogum/playlist/4RTsNbOE6cFgQMupXhc6gB">here</a>.</p>

<li id="comment-8146241">
    <div class="comment-inner-wrapper blue">
        <div class="comment-inner-inner-wrapper">
            <div class="comment-inner3-wrapper">
                <div class="comment-inner4-wrapper">
                    <div class="comment-inner5-wrapper">
                        <div class="comment-inner6-wrapper">
                            <div class="comment-avatar-box">
                                                                    <div class="avb">
                                                                                    
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        
                                            <div class="avatar-box-30">
                                                                                                                                                                                                                <img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/7b9862ea9cdfef2536e1e1ff9b8719d0?s=30&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.stereogum.com%2Fwp-content%2Fplugins%2Fwp-pi-comments%2Fimages%2Fdefault-user-avatar-30.png%3F_v%3D1fb0e64ac0fe89063aa0480ace86cb3b%26s%3D30&amp;r=G' class='avatar avatar-30 photo' height='30' width='30' />

                                                                        Jim Rountree
                                </div>

                                
                                
                                <div class="before-comment-text"></div>
                                <div class="comment-text">
                                    <p>allow me to explain: this list was a beast, i applaud you for trying. but do you really consider some of these songs their absolute best? i know in the article you wrote &#8220;these may not even be my picks for the best pink floyd songs&#8221;, and yet the ones you picked are the ones that everyone is uber-familiar with. you can&#8217;t possibly put another brick part 2 seriously as one of their best even from that album. 95% of the songs on that list are being played on classic rock stations as we speak, and you culled your list from basically four albums, from a band who&#8217;ve put out almost 20. don&#8217;t get me wrong, i think a list of this undertaking is hard, and NOBODY is going to please everybody, but this list is fairly safe. wish you were here, another brick part 2, comfortably numb, money?!, i mean, you gotta admit the list is really classic rocks radio-y. there&#8217;s no fucking way money goes on there but us and them doesn&#8217;t. there&#8217;s no way comfortably numb goes on there but fearless doesn&#8217;t. or one of these days. or mother. </p>
<p>also, this was mentioned somewhere else on the list, but nothing from obscured by clouds? or really anything with syd barrett? nothing from atom heart mother? fat old sun is one of the best guitar parts david gilmoure ever wrote. i&#8217;m glad echoes and shine on made the list, but come on, those are really easy calls too. echoes simply for the sheer bombasity of it. if you want to shake the classic rock radioness off of it, there are tons of other calls that could have gone on here, but another brick in the wall part 2, a song that deaf people could sing in their sleep thanks to it being overplayed, got put on there. i&#8217;m really not trying to insult you or anything, i think dogs, echoes, astronomy, even wish you were here, and shine, those are great songs and deserve consideration and placement, but money? that album is incredible and you picked the one song on there that does NOT need to anywhere near a best of pink floyd list, especially considering the very next song is the BEST song on that album. </p>
<p>anyway, sorry, i quit smoking two days ago and that&#8217;s the reason i wrote all this shit out like that. it&#8217;s like there are dogs barking and i&#8217;m the only one that can hear them. i&#8217;ve been eating like a horse and i don&#8217;t know what to do with my hands anymore.</p>
                                                                    </div>

    <div class="comment-inner-wrapper blue">
        <div class="comment-inner-inner-wrapper">
            <div class="comment-inner3-wrapper">
                <div class="comment-inner4-wrapper">
                    <div class="comment-inner5-wrapper">

                                            <div class="avatar-box-30">
                                                                                                                                                                                                                <img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/7b9862ea9cdfef2536e1e1ff9b8719d0?s=30&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.stereogum.com%2Fwp-content%2Fplugins%2Fwp-pi-comments%2Fimages%2Fdefault-user-avatar-30.png%3F_v%3D1fb0e64ac0fe89063aa0480ace86cb3b%26s%3D30&amp;r=G' class='avatar avatar-30 photo' height='30' width='30' />

                                <div class="before-comment-text"></div>
                                <div class="comment-text">
                                    <p>okay, well let me explain what i mean. first, i applaud you for making this list, it&#8217;s a toughy, and i recognize there is no way you&#8217;d be able to please everyone all the time with it. it&#8217;s fucking impossible. so i&#8217;m not going to criticize the list for that. but come on, some of these picks are straight out of the rotation of tampa&#8217;s classic rock lunch hour. another brick part 2? money? you said yourself that you don&#8217;t even know if this is really your favorites list, but look at what you decided to go with. 90% of it can be heard in regular rotation on the radio. you can&#8217;t tell me another brick in the wall part 2 deserves to be on that list while fearless, a FAR superior song to virtually every song on here is left off. money is on there but the next song on dark side, EASILY the best song on that album, us and them, doesn&#8217;t make the cut. comfortably numb? while good, any number of songs off of the wall would be better picks: mother, goodbye blue sky, run like hell, etc. none of the songs you picked are bad, but they cover the same ground that lazy classic rock computers pick out every day, and they cover the same four albums starting from dark side and ending with the wall. this is one era out of many that they had, and it&#8217;s the one everyone knows to death. even picking echoes and shine on, two POWERHOUSE songs that i agree with, is fairly predictable. echoes of course would go on there, simply because of it&#8217;s sheer bombastity. fearless, one of these days, careful with that axe eugene, stay, childhood&#8217;s end, the nile song, mother, us and them, brain damage, welcome to the machine (a song so stunning and utterly full of bile that i sometimes can&#8217;t believe they got it onto the album), all of these have places over the songs selected. or not, but at least they&#8217;re not on the radio.</p>
<p>also, look. i quite smoking two days ago, and this shit sucks. i don&#8217;t know what to do with my hands at all and i&#8217;m eating like a horse. nothing is safe, i&#8217;m one step away from pouring garlic powder down my throat. this is awful.</p>

<li id="comment-8145621">
    <div class="comment-inner-wrapper blue">
        <div class="comment-inner-inner-wrapper">
            <div class="comment-inner3-wrapper">
                <div class="comment-inner4-wrapper">
                    <div class="comment-inner5-wrapper">
                        <div class="comment-inner6-wrapper">
                            <div class="comment-avatar-box">
                                                                    <div class="avb">
                                                                                    
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        
                                            <div class="avatar-box-50">
                                                                                                                                                                                                                <img src="http://stereogum.com/wp-content/avatars/842/thumb_200842.gif" width="50" height="50" />

                                                    
                                                                                            </div>

                                                                                                                        </div>
                                                            </div>

                            <div class="comment-content">
                                                                <div class="comment-meta">
                                                                        homesickalien
                                </div>

                                
                                
                                <div class="before-comment-text"></div>
                                <div class="comment-text">
                                    <p>You almost have a great list here.  It&#8217;s not fair to pick top songs with this band.  They base their existence on concept and maybe one song separated from an album is fun to ponder, but taken as whole is the only way to realistically go here. </p>
<p>I think it&#8217;s only practical to include Arnold Layne, I mean, it&#8217;s sorta what got everyone&#8217;s attention.  Or, See Emily Play.  &#8220;astronomy&#8221; is surely the sexy pick here, but we need to acknowledge a songs place in their popularity.  I mean, we all love Dig a Pony, but Twist and Shout put The Beatles on the map, right?</p>
<p>&#8220;If&#8221; from Atom Heart Mother would be another great early pick.  Then again, the fact that nothing from Ummagumma is represented here is a travesty.</p>
<p>Although technically, The Final Cut was more or less a Roger Waters album, still songs like Gunner&#8217;s Dream, The Final Cut and Not Now John are some of the best examples of Gilmore&#8217;s guitar and Waters emotional songwriting working in an incredible collaborate effort.  They despised each other at this point in their careers, and the pain drips off each track. </p>
<p>Echoes is an obvious great choice, but to not mention &#8220;One of These Days&#8221; is a travesty.  I often think there is great similarity between that and Radioheads&#8217;, &#8220;National Anthem&#8221; for some reason.</p>
<p>Pigs &amp; Dogs are great but Sheep is as good or better.  Impossible to rank these apart.  Animals as an album should maybe be treated as just one long song.  Christ, Pigs on a Wing alone is 2 minutes of sheer perfection.</p>
<p>The Wall, oh The Wall&#8230;where to begin here.  First, having ABITW Pt2 and &#8216;Numb&#8217; the pick from here is good (obviously) but not it&#8217;s best representation.  &#8216;Mother,&#8217; &#8216;Hey You&#8217; Run Like Hell, and on and on and on&#8230;</p>
<p>&#8220;Us and Them&#8221; not included is a bit disturbing.  Also, &#8216;Time&#8217; includes one of Gilmore&#8217;s most underrated guitar solos.</p>
<p>From Wish You Were Here, I cannot accept the non mention of Welcome to the Machine.  So dark and brooding&#8230;so many songs have followed the formula used in this song.  Won&#8217;t even bring the argument of &#8216;Have a Cigar&#8217; to the table and its rock star finger pointing.</p>
<p>One thing that I think should also be awarded is the post break up Pink Floyd only because the name is still intact so the next chapter should be awarded.  I know there is great argument in the fact that Pink Floyd can only be what Waters and Gilmore created together, but the time of Momentary Lapse of Reason could have been an abrupt end to this incredible band, but they survived it, and songs like &#8216;The Dogs of War&#8217; and &#8216;Sorrow&#8217; still carried the overall energy of the band, regardless of who was or wasn&#8217;t there.  The same could be said with Waters and his &#8216;Pros and Cons&#8217; and &#8216;Radio KAOS&#8217; days but we all remember the war over their band name at the time, so I spose we must accept that.</p>
<p>I&#8217;m sure this was a hard one for you Doug and so respect your efforts here, but the only true way you could fire off a Pink Floyd list would be by album.</p>
                                                                    </div>
                                <div class="after-comment-text"></div>

<li id="comment-8145701">
    <div class="comment-inner-wrapper blue">
        <div class="comment-inner-inner-wrapper">
            <div class="comment-inner3-wrapper">
                <div class="comment-inner4-wrapper">
                    <div class="comment-inner5-wrapper">
                        <div class="comment-inner6-wrapper">
                            <div class="comment-avatar-box">
                                                                    <div class="avb">
                                                                                    
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        
                                            <div class="avatar-box-50">
                                                                                                                                                                                                                <img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/be82a26ccf523a9aea6c4478cdc9a2cf?s=50&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.stereogum.com%2Fwp-content%2Fplugins%2Fwp-pi-comments%2Fimages%2Fdefault-user-avatar-50.png%3F_v%3D1fb0e64ac0fe89063aa0480ace86cb3b%26s%3D50&amp;r=G' class='avatar avatar-50 photo' height='50' width='50' />

                                                    
                                                                                            </div>

                                                                                                                        </div>
                                                            </div>

                            <div class="comment-content">
                                                                <div class="comment-meta">
                                                                        Bryan Mack
                                </div>

                                
                                
                                <div class="before-comment-text"></div>
                                <div class="comment-text">
                                    <p>I likewise would like to push the love for Fearless.  It&#8217;s hard to argue against any of the tracks you&#8217;ve included, but I would say you unfairly relegate Obscured by Clouds to second-tier album status.  That one perfectly captures the moment between Meddle and Dark Side and is full of classics (and Free Four).</p>
<p>But I&#8217;m posting here to contend with your assertion that Dogs is the best guitar solo.  I would argue that the economic soloing on Wish You Were Here or the soaring flights on Comfortably Numb are better within the Floyd catalog.  However, best solo is from one of two songs in my book: either Fripp&#8217;s blissfully breaking robotics on St. Elmo&#8217;s Fire by Eno or, more probably, Tom Verlaine painting a sunrise with six strings on Marquee Moon.  The solo they captured on Marquee Moon is nothing short of pure joy.</p>
                                                                    </div>
                                <div class="after-comment-text"></div>

                                                                <div class="comment-meta">
                                                                        John Mabery
                                </div>

                                
                                
                                <div class="before-comment-text"></div>
                                <div class="comment-text">
                                    <p>Great job on a very serious undertaking.  It reminded me that time hasn&#8217;t softened Pink Floyd&#8217;s impact on my life, nor will I allow their music to be saturated by classic rock radio.  Even after I had been blown away years before the first time I listened to &#8220;Dark Side,&#8221; I was rocked just as hard by &#8220;Animals&#8221; and some of their more obscure avant garde works.  Sometimes you just have to submit to the fact that classics are still classics, no matter how many times we&#8217;ve heard them.  </p>
<p>By the way, not too many classic rock stations play &#8220;Dogs,&#8221; a tune that I think rightfully deserved to be high on this list.</p>
                                                                    </div>
                                <div class="after-comment-text"></div>

<p>One of These Days should have been on this list, though. In fact, it&#8217;s sole lyric (One of These Days I&#8217;m Going to Cut You Into Little Pieces&#8230;) is on my short list of greatest lyrics ever (right below JC&#8217;s &#8220;I Shot a Man in Reno, Just to Watch Him Die.&#8221;)</p>
<p>Also, I would put Astronomy Domine at the top rather than at the bottom.</p>

                                                                                                                                                                                                                <img alt='' src='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/0a33d298d7dc7dc783a42a9eecccbfa6?s=50&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.stereogum.com%2Fwp-content%2Fplugins%2Fwp-pi-comments%2Fimages%2Fdefault-user-avatar-50.png%3F_v%3D1fb0e64ac0fe89063aa0480ace86cb3b%26s%3D50&amp;r=G' class='avatar avatar-50 photo' height='50' width='50' />

                                                                <div class="comment-meta">
                                                                        teletronium
                                </div>

                                
                                
                                <div class="before-comment-text"></div>
                                <div class="comment-text">
                                    <p>I am probably the biggest Floyd Head on the planet. This is not an easy task to undertake however I think you did a great job and I like your descriptions for the most part. While my ten are not necessarily the ten listed, I think this still accurately describes the bulk of their work in a nutshell but we know you can&#8217;t fit pink floyd into a nutshell. While some classic rock snobs might argue what is best/ worst that is just conjecture. The only thing I would like to have read is more mention of Rick Wright&#8217;s work on Animals even though he doesn&#8217;t get writing credit. While Gilmore&#8217;s talk box work IS some of the best example of that medium, Animals is the album where Wright&#8217;s keyboard work shines from nice Rhodes intros to Waters voice cross fading into minimoog madness. Nice Work! I was skeptical at the beginning but I&#8217;m glad I read through till the end.</p>
                                                                    </div>
                                <div class="after-comment-text"></div>
